import torch
from transformers import AutoConfig, AutoModelForCausalLM


class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits


model_name = "facebook/opt-1.3b"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 512
batch_size = 8


def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

If we load the entire K and V matrices into shared memory for each block: - K: 512 * 64 floats = 32,768 floats = 131KB - V: 512 * 64 floats = 131KB - Total: 262KB > 227KB limit! So we can't fit both K and V entirely in shared memory. We need to tile. Alternatively, we can load K into shared memory, compute all Q@K^T and store the scores in shared memory, then load V and compute the output. But 512 * 512 float scores = 1MB, which doesn't fit in shared memory either. So we need a different approach. Maybe: 1. Tile over the sequence dimension 2. Each block computes a tile of the output 3. Or use a different algorithm For seq_len=512 and head_dim=64, we could: 1. Each block handles one output position (thread handles dot products) 2. Load Q[i, :] into registers 3. Stream K and V through shared memory in chunks 4. Accumulate For example, chunk size = 64: - Load K[j:j+64, :] into shared memory: 64 * 64 * 4 = 16KB - Compute dot products for Q[i, :] with each K chunk - Load V[j:j+64, :] into shared memory: 16KB - Accumulate weighted sum Actually, this is similar to Flash Attention's tiling strategy but much simpler since we don't need to handle very long sequences. Let me sketch a simpler kernel: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { // One block per (batch, head) pair // Threads within block handle different output positions int tid = threadIdx.x; int bid = blockIdx.x; // Offset for this (batch, head) q += bid * seq_len * head_dim; k += bid * seq_len * head_dim; v += bid * seq_len * head_dim; out += bid * seq_len * head_dim; if (tid >= seq_len) return; // Load Q[tid, :] into registers float q_vec[64]; // head_dim = 64 for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_vec[d] = q[tid * head_dim + d]; } // Compute attention scores with all keys float scores[512]; // seq_len = 512 float max_score = -1e20f; for (int j = 0; j < seq_len; j++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_vec[d] * k[j * head_dim + d]; } float score = dot / sqrtf(head_dim); scores[j] = score; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } // Compute softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < seq_len; j++) { scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[j]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int j = 0; j < seq_len; j++) { scores[j] *= inv_sum; } // Compute weighted sum of values for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float out_val = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < seq_len; j++) { out_val += scores[j] * v[j * head_dim + d]; } out[tid * head_dim + d] = out_val; } } ``` Wait, this uses `scores[512]` which is 2KB per thread. With 512 threads, that's 1MB of shared memory if stored there, but as thread-local arrays, it goes into registers or local memory. 2KB per thread with 512 threads = 1MB total. If this spills to local memory (which is global memory), performance will be terrible. Hmm, with 64K registers per SM and 255 registers per thread max: - 512 threads * 255 registers = ~130K registers, which is more than 64K per SM - So we'd be limited to about 128 threads per block (64K / ~500 registers per thread) or register spilling Actually wait, I think I'm overcomplicating this. Each thread doesn't need to store all 512 scores. We can process in chunks: ```cuda // Each thread handles one output position // We process keys and values in chunks, applying softmax in a numerically stable way // Online softmax approach: // - Process chunk of scores // - Update max and sum incrementally // - Then process values in chunks using the final softmax weights ``` But actually for a correct implementation, we need all scores to compute softmax, then all values to compute the weighted sum. With seq_len=512, each thread could: 1. Compute all scores and keep the max 2. Do a second pass to compute exp(score - max) and accumulate sum 3. Do a third pass over values to compute output For step 1 and 2, we don't need to store all scores. We just need the max and can compute contributions to the sum in a single pass. For step 3, we DO need the softmax weights to multiply with V. But we can recompute them! The cost of recomputing scores is the same as loading them from memory (actually, recomputing avoids a memory read). So the kernel becomes: 1. Load Q[i, :] into registers 2. First pass: compute scores, track max_score 3. Second pass: compute exp(score - max_score), track sum_exp 4. Third pass: compute weighted sum of V using freshly computed softmax weights Wait, but then we're computing Q@K^T three times. That's 3x the compute. For head_dim=64 and seq_len=512, each pass is 32K FLOPs. Three passes is 96K FLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS, that's 1.4 microseconds. Negligible! And we save storing 512 scores per thread. This is a great tradeoff. Let me refine this approach. Actually for the third pass, we need the already-computed softmax weights. But we can recompute them: ``` for each j: score = dot(Q[i], K[j]) / sqrt(d) weight = exp(score - max_score) / sum_exp for each d: out[d] += weight * V[j][d] ``` So the algorithm per thread is: 1. Find max_score over all j 2. Compute sum_exp over all j 3. Compute output as weighted sum, recomputing weight for each j This is O(3 * seq_len * head_dim) compute with O(seq_len * head_dim) memory reads of K and V. Actually wait, I still need to think about register usage. With head_dim=64, and storing q_vec[64], that's 64 registers. Plus some for accumulation and loop counters. Total maybe ~100 registers per thread. With 512 threads per block, that's 51,200 registers. Each SM has 64K registers, so about 1.2 blocks per SM. With 132 SMs on H100, we can run ~158 blocks concurrently, which is less than our 256 blocks (8*32). So we'd need 2 waves. But that's fine. Actually, the occupancy might be a limiting factor. 512 threads with 100 registers each = 51,200 / 65,536 = ~78% occupancy. That's decent but not great. We could reduce threads per block to 256 and have each thread handle 2 output positions. Then 256 threads * 100 registers = 25,600 registers, allowing 2 blocks per SM. Total blocks = 8 * 32 = 256, with 132 SMs * 2 = 264 concurrent blocks. That fits in one wave! But then each thread has more work (2 positions instead of 1). Actually, this could be good for instruction-level parallelism. Let me think about this more. Actually, for an H100, the exact occupancy matters less because it's a massive GPU. Let me write one that's actually useful. Here's what I'll do: write a custom kernel for **fused residual addition + layer normalization**. This is a common fusion point: ```cuda __global__ void fused_residual_layernorm( float* out, const float* hidden, const float* residual, const float* weight, const float* bias, int hidden_size ) { // Each block handles one token // Computes: out = layer_norm(hidden + residual) } ``` This saves one memory round-trip for reading/writing the residual addition output. For batch=8, seq=512, hidden=2048: - Residual add: read hidden (16MB) + read residual (16MB) + write temp (16MB) = 48MB traffic - LayerNorm: read temp (16MB) + write out (16MB) = 32MB traffic - Total without fusion: 80MB per occurrence - With fusion: 48MB per occurrence - Savings: 32MB per LN There are 2 LNs per layer + 1 final LN = 49 LNs total. - Total savings: 49 * 32MB = 1.56GB - At 3.35 TB/s: ~0.5ms saved Hmm, not very impactful. What about **fused attention**? Let me calculate the savings there more carefully. Without fusion, per layer: - Q@K^T: read Q, K; write scores - Softmax: read scores; write softmax - @V: read softmax, V; write output - Score matrix: 8*32*512*512*4 = 268MB - Total traffic: 2 * 268MB (read+write) + 2 * 268MB (read+write) = 1072MB? Actually... For Q@K^T: read Q and K, write scores. Q: 8*32*512*64*4=33.5MB, K: 33.5MB, scores: 268MB. Total: 335MB For softmax: read scores, write softmax. 268MB + 268MB = 536MB? No, in-place softmax reuses memory. Read scores, write in-place. 268MB each = 536MB traffic. For @V: read softmax and V, write output. 268MB + 33.5MB + 33.5MB = 335MB. Total: 335 + 536 + 335 = 1.2GB per layer. With fused attention: read Q, K, V; write output. 33.5 + 33.5 + 33.5 + 33.5 = 134MB. Savings: ~1.07GB per layer. For 24 layers: ~25.7GB saved! At 3.35 TB/s: ~7.7ms saved. This is much more significant! So the fused attention kernel is worth writing. Now, is 7.7ms enough to beat the reference? If the reference takes 100-200ms, then 7.7ms is a 4-8% speedup. Not huge, but combined with other optimizations it could help. Wait, I made an error. The softmax might be computed in-place, so write traffic is halved. Let me recalculate: Without fusion (per layer): - Q@K^T: read Q(33.5MB) + read K(33.5MB) + write scores(268MB) = 335MB - Softmax (in-place): read scores(268MB) + write scores(268MB) = 536MB - @V: read scores(268MB) + read V(33.5MB) + write out(33.5MB) = 335MB Total: 1206MB per layer Hmm, that seems high. Actually, for the softmax, if it's done in shared memory or registers within the kernel, it might not write to global memory twice. But in practice, PyTorch's softmax kernel reads and writes global memory. Actually, I think PyTorch operations are fused when possible by the PyTorch JIT. For example, `softmax(Q @ K^T / sqrt(d)) @ V` might actually be computed as separate kernel launches but the softmax might be done in registers with `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention`. Looking at the transformers source code, `OPTAttention` uses: ```python attn_weights = torch.matmul(query_states, key_states.transpose(-1, -2)) / self.scale attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1) attn_weights = nn.functional.dropout(attn_weights, ...) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_weights, value_states) ``` It does NOT use SDPA by default! SDPA is only used if explicitly configured with `attn_implementation="sdpa"`. The default is the eager implementation. So there IS an opportunity to optimize attention. Let me now think about whether there are other simple optimizations I can apply: 1. **Use `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention` instead of my custom kernel** - but this defeats the custom kernel requirement 2. **Pre-transpose all weight matrices** - so we can use `torch.mm` without transpose. Actually, cuBLAS handles this efficiently. 3. **Use `torch.addmm` for bias + matmul** - saves a kernel launch 4. **Fuse GELU + down projection** - requires custom GEMM 5. **Use `torch.jit.script` or `torch.compile`** - might help but not a custom CUDA kernel Let me think about what else I can write a custom kernel for. What about writing a kernel that handles **multiple attention heads in parallel** more efficiently? For example, instead of calling my custom attention kernel once per head or with a single big kernel, I could write it to process all heads simultaneously. OK, let me just write a practical implementation. For the attention kernel, I'll write one that: - Takes Q, K, V as contiguous tensors of shape [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] - Outputs attention of shape [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] - Uses a simple tile-based algorithm Here is my kernel design: ``` // Grid: (batch * num_heads) blocks // Block: 256 or 512 threads, handling different seq positions // Each block processes one (batch, head) pair // Threads in the block handle different output sequence positions // Algorithm per thread (handling one or more seq positions): // 1. For each output position i: // a. Load Q[i, :] into registers // b. Compute scores with all K[j, :], track max_score // c. Compute softmax denominator (sum of exp) // d. Compute weighted sum of V[j, :] // e. Write output[i, :] ``` For seq_len=512 and head_dim=64: - If we use 512 threads per block, each thread handles exactly 1 output position. - Q[i,:] is 64 floats, stored in registers. - We iterate over all 512 keys, computing dot products. - Then iterate again to compute softmax weights and accumulate V. Actually, recomputing scores twice might be cheaper than storing them. Let me compute: - Computing score: 64 multiplies + 63 adds = ~127 FLOPs - For 512 keys: ~65K FLOPs - Doing this twice: ~130K FLOPs - On H100 at 67 TFLOPS: ~2 microseconds - Loading 512 scores from global memory: 512 * 4 = 2KB - At 3.35 TB/s: ~0.6 nanoseconds per thread, but with memory coalescing, for 512 threads = ~0.3 microseconds - So recomputing adds ~1.7 microseconds vs storing Given that storing scores also consumes registers/shared memory that could limit occupancy, recomputing seems reasonable. But actually, we can do better. We can use the **online softmax** algorithm which processes in a single pass but needs a final correction pass: ``` max = -inf sum = 0 for j in range(seq_len): score = dot(Q[i], K[j]) if score > max: sum = sum * exp(max - score) + 1 max = score else: sum = sum + exp(score - max) # Now max and sum are correct output = 0 for j in range(seq_len): score = dot(Q[i], K[j]) weight = exp(score - max) / sum output += weight * V[j] ``` Wait, that's not quite right. Let me think about online softmax properly. Actually, for the standard softmax, we need: ``` softmax(x_i) = exp(x_i - max) / sum_j(exp(x_j - max)) ``` We can compute `max` in one pass. Then compute `sum` in a second pass. Then compute the output in a third pass. That's what I described earlier. Can we combine pass 2 and 3? We could compute the output incrementally, but then if we find a new maximum later, we'd need to reweight all previous contributions. So online softmax for the final weighted sum is tricky unless we store intermediate sums. The standard online softmax algorithm (used in Flash Attention) does: 1. Keep track of running max `m` and running sum `l` 2. For each new chunk: - Compute new max `m_new = max(m, max_of_chunk)` - Update sum: `l_new = exp(m - m_new) * l + sum(exp(chunk - m_new))` 3. At the end, you have the correct max and sum For the weighted sum of values, we can do: 1. Keep track of running max `m`, running sum `l`, running output `o` 2. For each new chunk of keys and values: - Compute scores for the chunk - Find `m_new = max(m, max_of_chunk_scores)` - For the output: `o_new = exp(m - m_new) * o + matmul(exp(chunk_scores - m_new), chunk_values)` - Update `l_new = exp(m - m_new) * l + sum(exp(chunk_scores - m_new))` 3. At the end: `output = o_new / l_new` This is the exact algorithm used in Flash Attention! And it only requires one pass over the data, not three. But it requires carrying `o` (the output vector of size head_dim) as state. For head_dim=64, each thread carries: - `q_vec[64]`: 64 floats - `o[64]`: 64 floats - `m`: 1 float - `l`: 1 float Total: ~130 floats = 520 bytes per thread With 256 threads per block: 133KB of registers. With 64K registers per SM (each register is 4 bytes), that's 256KB of register file total. So we'd use about half. Pretty good! And this processes keys and values in a single pass! The algorithm: ``` o = zeros(head_dim) m = -inf l = 0 for each chunk of K, V: s = dot(Q[i], K_chunk) // scores for this chunk m_new = max(m, max(s)) p = exp(s - m_new) // unnormalized probs l_new = exp(m - m_new) * l + sum(p) // Update output: scale old output and add new contribution o = exp(m - m_new) * o + p^T @ V_chunk m = m_new l = l_new output = o / l ``` Wait, `p^T @ V_chunk` is a weighted sum of `V_chunk`. For a chunk of size `B_c`, this is: - `p` is a vector of size `B_c` - `V_chunk` is a matrix of size `B_c × head_dim` - The result is a vector of size `head_dim` This is essentially a matrix-vector product. Each thread can compute this directly. For chunk size = 64: - We load K_chunk into shared memory: 64 * 64 * 4 = 16KB - We load V_chunk into shared memory: 64 * 64 * 4 = 16KB - Total shared memory: 32KB per block - Compute scores: dot(Q[i], K_chunk[j]) for j=0..63 - Compute weighted sum of V_chunk This is very efficient! And it only needs one pass over K and V. Let me refine this. I'll divide the sequence into chunks. Each thread handles one output position i. Within the block, threads can share the loaded K_chunk and V_chunk through shared memory. ```cuda #define SEQ_LEN 512 #define HEAD_DIM 64 #define CHUNK_SIZE 64 __global__ void flash_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { int batch = blockIdx.x / num_heads; int head = blockIdx.x % num_heads; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Base offsets for this (batch, head) int bh_offset = ((batch * num_heads + head) * seq_len * head_dim); q += bh_offset; k += bh_offset; v += bh_offset; out += bh_offset; // Each thread handles one sequence position i int i = tid; if (i >= seq_len) return; // Load Q[i, :] into registers float q_vec[HEAD_DIM]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_vec[d] = q[i * head_dim + d]; } // Running statistics float m = -1e20f; float l = 0.0f; float o[HEAD_DIM]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { o[d] = 0.0f; } // Shared memory for K_chunk and V_chunk extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; // [CHUNK_SIZE][HEAD_DIM] float* v_smem = smem + CHUNK_SIZE * head_dim; // [CHUNK_SIZE][HEAD_DIM] float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); // Iterate over chunks for (int chunk_start = 0; chunk_start < seq_len; chunk_start += CHUNK_SIZE) { int chunk_end = min(chunk_start + CHUNK_SIZE, seq_len); int chunk_size = chunk_end - chunk_start; // Collaborative loading of K_chunk and V_chunk into shared memory for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < chunk_size * head_dim; idx += blockDim.x) { int j = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; k_smem[j * head_dim + d] = k[(chunk_start + j) * head_dim + d]; v_smem[j * head_dim + d] = v[(chunk_start + j) * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute scores for this chunk float s[CHUNK_SIZE]; float m_chunk = -1e20f; for (int j = 0; j < chunk_size; j++) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_vec[d] * k_smem[j * head_dim + d]; } s[j] = dot * scale; if (s[j] > m_chunk) m_chunk = s[j]; } // Global max float m_new = fmaxf(m, m_chunk); // Compute correction factors and update sum float exp_m_old = expf(m - m_new); float l_new = l * exp_m_old; for (int j = 0; j < chunk_size; j++) { float p = expf(s[j] - m_new); l_new += p; } // Update output for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { o[d] *= exp_m_old; } for (int j = 0; j < chunk_size; j++) { float p = expf(s[j] - m_new); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { o[d] += p * v_smem[j * head_dim + d]; } } m = m_new; l = l_new; __syncthreads(); } // Normalize and write output float inv_l = 1.0f / l; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out[i * head_dim + d] = o[d] * inv_l; } } ``` Wait, there are some issues with this kernel: 1. `s[CHUNK_SIZE]` with `CHUNK_SIZE=64` uses only 64 floats per thread, which is fine. 2. However, each block handles one (batch, head), and with `256` threads per block, we'd only process 256 of the 512 sequence positions. We'd need 2 blocks per (batch, head), or use 512 threads. This would spill to local memory. Actually, `s[64]` alone is 64 floats = 256 bytes. With 512 threads, if stored in registers that's 131KB. Let me draft the kernel more carefully: ```cuda __global__ void flash_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { int batch = blockIdx.x / num_heads; int head = blockIdx.x % num_heads; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; int bh_offset = ((batch * num_heads + head) * seq_len * head_dim); q += bh_offset; k += bh_offset; v += bh_offset; out += bh_offset; // Each thread handles multiple positions int positions_per_thread = (seq_len + num_threads - 1) / num_threads; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + CHUNK_SIZE * head_dim; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); for (int pos_idx = 0; pos_idx < positions_per_thread; pos_idx++) { int i = tid + pos_idx * num_threads; if (i >= seq_len) continue; // Load Q[i, :] into registers float q_vec[HEAD_DIM]; chunk_start < seq_len; chunk_start += CHUNK_SIZE) { int chunk_end = min(chunk_start + CHUNK_SIZE, seq_len); int chunk_size = chunk_end - chunk_start; // Collaborative load for (int idx = tid; idx < chunk_size * head_dim; idx += num_threads) { int j = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; k_smem[j * head_dim + d] = k[(chunk_start + j) * head_dim + d]; v_smem[j * head_dim + d] = v[(chunk_start + j) * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); For the attention module: ```python class OPTAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, ...): self.q_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim) self.k_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim) self.v_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim) def forward(self, hidden_states, ...): query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) key_states = self.k_proj(hidden_states) value_states = self.v_proj(hidden_states) # Reshape for multi-head attention query_states = query_states.view(...).transpose(1, 2) key_states = key_states.view(...).transpose(1, 2) value_states = value_states.view(...).transpose(1, 2) # Attention attn_weights = torch.matmul(query_states, key_states.transpose(-1, -2)) / self.scaling if attention_mask is not None: attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1, dtype=torch.float32) attn_weights = nn.functional.dropout(...) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_weights, value_states) # Reshape back attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2).reshape(...) attn_output = self.out_proj(attn_output) return attn_output, ... ``` Wait, for the causal language model at training time, there might be an attention mask to prevent attending to future tokens. At inference time for the full sequence, yes there is a causal mask. Let me check... Actually, for the forward pass of OPT, the attention mask is typically created as a causal mask: ```python attention_mask = _prepare_4d_causal_attention_mask(...) ``` This mask adds a very large negative value to positions that shouldn't be attended to, making them zero after softmax. In the HuggingFace implementation, the mask is added to the attention scores: ```python attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask ``` where `attention_mask` contains 0 for valid positions and a large negative number for masked positions. For our custom kernel, we need to implement this causal masking. For position i, tokens j > i should be masked out. In our attention kernel, for each output position i: - We compute scores with all keys j=0..seq_len-1 - For j > i, we should set the score to -inf before softmax This is easy to add: ```cuda if (j > i) { s[j] = -1e20f; // or some large negative number } ``` Or more precisely, we can just skip computing those scores or set them to -inf. For causal attention, the mask is triangular: position i can only attend to positions 0..i. Let me modify the kernel to handle causal masking. This is essential for correct output. Also, in the HF implementation, the attention weights are computed in FP32, then cast back. And there's a scaling factor of `1 / sqrt(head_dim)`. For head_dim=64, that's 1/8. One more thing: for the attention mask in HF, it's typically created with `torch.finfo(torch.float32).min` which is about -3.4e38. We can use a similarly large negative number. Actually, for numerical stability in our kernel, we can simply not include j > i in the softmax computation. We can treat it as the score being -inf, which means exp(score) = 0, which means they contribute nothing to the sum. But wait - in our online algorithm, if we set scores for j > i to -inf, then: - For the max computation, these -inf values don't affect the max - For the sum, exp(-inf) = 0, so they don't contribute - For the output, their weight is 0 So we can simply check `if (j > i) continue;` when computing scores. But we also need `j` to be the actual sequence index, not the chunk-local index. Let me update the kernel with causal masking. For our manual implementation, we'll use `F.linear(input, weight, bias)` which includes bias. Now let me also think about the attention mask more carefully. Still, we should handle the causal mask correctly. In the HF implementation, for a standard causal mask, positions are masked as: - `attention_mask[i, j] = 0` if j <= i (lower triangular including diagonal) - `attention_mask[i, j] = very_negative` if j > i (upper triangular) Actually wait, I need to be more careful. In the HF implementation, the causal mask is typically: - For position i in the sequence, positions j > i are masked (i.e., can't attend to future tokens) - So `attention_mask[i, j] = 0` for j <= i, and `-inf` for j > i Yes, that's standard causal masking for autoregressive models. In our kernel, for output position i, we should only attend to keys at positions j where j <= i. Let me also check: does OPT use any special attention scaling? Looking at the code: ```python attn_weights = torch.bmm(query_states, key_states.transpose(1, 2)) * self.scaling ``` where `self.scaling = self.head_dim ** -0.5`. Yes, standard scaling. One subtlety: in HF's implementation, the softmax is computed in float32: ```python attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1, dtype=torch.float32).to(query_states.dtype) ``` But since our input is already float32, this doesn't matter. OK I think I have enough understanding. Let me now write the actual kernel and model code. Let me draft the CUDA code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #define SEQ_LEN 512 #define HEAD_DIM 64 #define CHUNK_SIZE 64 // For shared memory tiling __global__ void fused_attention_forward( const float* __restrict__ query, const float* __restrict__ key, const float* __restrict__ value, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { int batch = blockIdx.x / num_heads; int head = blockIdx.x % num_heads; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; int bh_offset = ((batch * num_heads + head) * seq_len * head_dim); query += bh_offset; key += bh_offset; value += bh_offset; output += bh_offset; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + CHUNK_SIZE * head_dim; // Each thread handles multiple positions int positions_per_thread = (seq_len + num_threads - 1) / num_threads; for (int p = 0; p < positions_per_thread; p++) { int i = tid + p * num_threads; if (i >= seq_len) continue; // Load Q[i, :] into registers float q_vec[HEAD_DIM]; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_vec[d] = query[i * head_dim + d]; } float m = -1e20f; float l = 0.0f; float o[HEAD_DIM]; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { o[d] = 0.0f; } // Iterate over key/value chunks for (int chunk_start = 0; chunk_start < seq_len; chunk_start += CHUNK_SIZE) { int chunk_end = min(chunk_start + CHUNK_SIZE, seq_len); int chunk_size = chunk_end - chunk_start; // All threads collaboratively load K and V chunks into shared memory int total_load = chunk_size * head_dim; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_load; idx += num_threads) { int j = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; k_smem[j * head_dim + d] = key[(chunk_start + j) * head_dim + d]; v_smem[j * head_dim + d] = value[(chunk_start + j) * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute scores for this chunk float m_chunk = -1e20f; float s[CHUNK_SIZE]; for (int j = 0; j < chunk_size; j++) { int seq_j = chunk_start + j; if (seq_j > i) { s[j] = -1e20f; continue; } float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_vec[d] * k_smem[j * head_dim + d]; } s[j] = dot * scale; if (s[j] > m_chunk) m_chunk = s[j]; } // Online softmax update float m_new = fmaxf(m, m_chunk); float exp_m_old = expf(m - m_new); float l_new = l * exp_m_old; for (int j = 0; j < chunk_size; j++) { l_new += expf(s[j] - m_new); } for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { o[d] *= exp_m_old; } for (int j = 0; j < chunk_size; j++) { float p = expf(s[j] - m_new); for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { o[d] += p * v_smem[j * head_dim + d]; } } m = m_new; l = l_new; __syncthreads(); } // Write output float inv_l = 1.0f / l; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { output[i * head_dim + d] = o[d] * inv_l; } } } torch::Tensor attention_forward( torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor key, torch::Tensor value ) { int batch_size = query.size(0); int num_heads = query.size(1); int seq_len = query.size(2); int head_dim = query.size(3); auto output = torch::empty_like(query); int num_blocks = batch_size * num_heads; int num_threads = 128; // Each thread handles up to 4 positions size_t smem_size = 2 * CHUNK_SIZE * head_dim * sizeof(float); fused_attention_forward<<<num_blocks, num_threads, smem_size>>>( query.data_ptr<float>(), key.data_ptr<float>(), value.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim ); return output; } ``` Wait, there are some issues with this kernel: 1. `__syncthreads()` inside the position loop might deadlock if some threads `continue` past the syncthreads. But in our case, all threads have work (for seq_len=512 and num_threads=128, all have exactly 4 positions). However, if seq_len is not a multiple of num_threads, some threads might have fewer positions and the last iteration `i >= seq_len` would `continue`, skipping the `__syncthreads()`. Actually, in the outer loop `for (int p = 0; p < positions_per_thread; p++)`, the first few iterations all threads have valid work. But for the LAST iteration, some threads might have `i >= seq_len`. If the valid threads enter `__syncthreads()` but the invalid threads don't, we get deadlock! To fix this, I should restructure so that ALL threads always participate in `__syncthreads()`, even if they don't have valid work. Option 1: Move `__syncthreads()` outside the position loop? No, because we need the sync between loading chunks and computing for each position. Option 2: Restructure so threads without work still execute `__syncthreads()`: ```cuda for (int p = 0; p < positions_per_thread; p++) { int i = tid + p * num_threads; bool valid = (i < seq_len); float q_vec[HEAD_DIM]; float o[HEAD_DIM]; float m, l; if (valid) { // Load Q for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_vec[d] = query[i * head_dim + d]; } m = -1e20f; l = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) o[d] = 0.0f; } for (int chunk_start = 0; chunk_start < seq_len; chunk_start += CHUNK_SIZE) { // All threads load __syncthreads(); if (valid) { // Compute for position i using loaded chunk } __syncthreads(); } if (valid) { // Write output } } ``` This works because ALL threads participate in both `__syncthreads()` calls. Wait, but the chunk loading also needs to happen before the first `__syncthreads()`. Let me restructure: ```cuda for (int chunk_start = 0; chunk_start < seq_len; chunk_start += CHUNK_SIZE) { int chunk_end = min(chunk_start + CHUNK_SIZE, seq_len); int chunk_size = chunk_end - chunk_start; // All threads load K and V chunks for (int idx = tid; idx < chunk_size * head_dim; idx += num_threads) { ... } __syncthreads(); // Each valid thread processes its positions for (int p = 0; p < positions_per_thread; p++) { int i = tid + p * num_threads; if (i >= seq_len) continue; // Compute using the loaded chunk // Update running stats for position i } __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, this has a problem too: if one thread handles multiple positions, it updates its running stats for all positions using the SAME loaded chunk before moving to the next chunk. This is correct! Because we process chunk_start..chunk_end for all positions. But then each thread needs to maintain SEPARATE running stats (m, l, o) for EACH of its positions. That means arrays of these values, one per position. For positions_per_thread=4: - `float m[4], l[4];` - `float o[4][HEAD_DIM];` - `float q_vec[4][HEAD_DIM];` That's 8 + 4 * 64 * 4 + 4 * 64 * 4 = 8 + 1024 + 1024 = 2056 bytes per thread. For 128 threads: ~262KB total. That's too much for registers! We can store `q_vec` and `o` in shared memory or keep them per-thread. But 262KB of register pressure would definitely spill. Alternative approach: process ONE position at a time, but store ALL positions' intermediate state in shared memory? No, that would be even more shared memory. Actually, wait. The issue is that if we load a chunk and process all positions, each position needs its own running stats. But if we process positions one at a time, we only need stats for one position. What if we load chunks and process them position by position? ```cuda // For each position assigned to this thread: for (int p = 0; p < positions_per_thread; p++) { int i = tid + p * num_threads; if (i >= seq_len) continue; // Skip but ensure we still sync later // Load Q[i, :] float q_vec[HEAD_DIM]; ... // For each chunk: for (int chunk_start = 0; chunk_start < seq_len; chunk_start += CHUNK_SIZE) { // Load K and V chunk (all threads participate) __syncthreads(); // Need this before loading // Load code... __syncthreads(); // Need this after loading // If valid, compute for this chunk if (i < seq_len) { // Update stats for position i } } // Write output for position i } ``` This is what I had before, but with the deadlock issue. Let me fix the deadlock issue explicitly: ```cuda for (int p = 0; p < positions_per_thread; p++) { int i = tid + p * num_threads; bool valid = (i < seq_len); float q_vec[HEAD_DIM]; float o[HEAD_DIM]; float m = -1e20f; float l = 0.0f; if (valid) { for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_vec[d] = query[i * head_dim + d]; o[d] = 0.0f; } } for (int chunk_start = 0; chunk_start < seq_len; chunk_start += CHUNK_SIZE) { int chunk_size = min(CHUNK_SIZE, seq_len - chunk_start); // All threads participate in loading for (int idx = tid; idx < chunk_size * head_dim; idx += num_threads) { int j = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; int seq_j = chunk_start + j; k_smem[j * head_dim + d] = key[seq_j * head_dim + d]; v_smem[j * head_dim + d] = value[seq_j * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); if (valid) { // Compute scores and update stats ... } __syncthreads(); } if (valid) { // Write output ... } } ``` This should work! Every thread hits both `__syncthreads()` calls in the chunk loop regardless of whether `valid` is true. Wait, but there's a potential issue with `q_vec` and `o`. Are they in registers? For `HEAD_DIM=64`, each array is 64 * 4 = 256 bytes. For 128 threads, that's 32KB of register pressure per array. With `q_vec`, `o`, plus some scalars and temporaries, maybe ~100KB total. That's manageable. But wait: what about the array `s[CHUNK_SIZE]`? For `CHUNK_SIZE=64`, that's 256 bytes per thread, another 32KB. Total maybe ~130KB. All within 256KB per block. OK, with 130KB per block and 256KB per SM, we get ~1.9 blocks per SM. Total concurrent blocks: 132 * 1.9 ≈ 251. We need 256 blocks, so it's almost one wave. Still good. Actually, some SMs can run 2 blocks and some can only run 1, depending on register allocation. The CUDA runtime handles this. But overall it should be close to 1 wave. OK, I think this kernel design is solid. Let me now think about other custom kernels I should write. For the overall speedup, besides attention, the main gains could come from: 1. Using `torch.inference_mode()` 2. Avoiding HF Transformers overhead 3. Any other fusion What if I also write a custom kernel for **layer normalization**? Actually, PyTorch's LayerNorm is already quite efficient. But I could write a **fused residual + layer norm** kernel. Let's calculate impact: - Without fusion: residual add writes temp (33.5MB), layernorm reads temp (33.5MB), writes normalized (33.5MB) - With fusion: read hidden (33.5MB), read residual (33.5MB), write normalized (33.5MB) - Saves: 33.5MB per fused operation For 49 such operations (2 per layer * 24 + 1 final): 49 * 33.5MB = 1.6GB At 3.35 TB/s: ~0.5ms saved Small but not zero. What about **fused bias + residual add** after attention/FFN output projections? - Without: linear writes output (33.5MB), bias add reads/writes (67MB), residual add reads/writes (100MB) - Wait, PyTorch linear includes bias, so: linear writes output+bias (33.5MB), then residual add reads/writes (67MB) - With fusion: linear writes output (33.5MB), we could add residual in the same kernel Actually, for the output projection from linear layers, PyTorch might fuse bias add in the GEMM. Then we only need to add the residual. Not much to gain there. What about **fused GELU + down projection** in the FFN? That would require implementing a GEMM, which is complex. Maybe `fused_layernorm` if needed Actually, `torch.add` is already very efficient. Writing a custom kernel for it won't help. I'll focus on the attention kernel. Wait, I just realized I should also think about whether the **Q, K, V projections** can be batched. In HF, they're separate `nn.Linear` layers. I could combine them into one larger projection: `[hidden, 3*hidden]` and then split the output. This might be slightly more efficient due to fewer kernel launches and potentially better GEMM efficiency. Similarly, we can do the same for the output projection and FFN. Actually, for QKV projection: - 3 separate matmuls: each is [4096, 2048] × [2048, 2048] - Batched as one: [4096, 2048] × [2048, 6144] The single larger matmul might have better GEMM efficiency. And we save 2 out of 3 bias additions (fused in the matmul anyway). This is a good optimization! PyTorch can do this with a single `nn.Linear(hidden_size, 3*hidden_size)` if we concatenate the weights. But wait, the original model has separate Q, K, V weights. In `__init__`, I can concatenate them: ```python qkv_weight = torch.cat([q_proj.weight, k_proj.weight, v_proj.weight], dim=0) qkv_bias = torch.cat([q_proj.bias, k_proj.bias, v_proj.bias], dim=0) ``` Then in forward: ```python qkv = F.linear(hidden_states, qkv_weight, qkv_bias) q, k, v = qkv.chunk(3, dim=-1) ``` This saves some overhead. But does it make things faster? The GEMM with output 6144 might be slightly faster than 3 GEMMs with output 2048 each because: - Larger GEMMs often have better occupancy and utilization - Fewer kernel launches - Still, cuBLAS is very efficient even for smaller GEMMs Let me estimate: - Single [4096, 2048] × [2048, 6144]: ~100 GFLOPs - Three [4096, 2048] × [2048, 2048]: ~33.5 GFLOPs each, total ~100 GFLOPs - Compute is the same, but the larger GEMM might run slightly more efficiently I'll include this optimization. Similarly, for the FFN, I can't fuse up and down projections since there's GELU in between. But I can keep them efficient. One more optimization: **pre-transpose weights** so that F.linear doesn't need to transpose internally. PyTorch's `F.linear(input, weight, bias)` computes `input @ weight.t() + bias`. The `.t()` is just a view and doesn't actually transpose memory, but cuBLAS handles transposed matrices. Still, we can explicitly store weights in the transposed format: ```python self.qkv_weight = nn.Parameter(qkv_weight.t().contiguous()) ``` Then use `torch.mm(input, self.qkv_weight)` which avoids the transpose in the linear call. Actually, `nn.Linear` already stores weight as `[out_features, in_features]` and does `input @ weight.t()`. If we manually transpose and store, we need to be careful. I'll just use `F.linear` which should be optimal. OK let me now write the complete implementation. I'll structure it as follows: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from transformers import AutoConfig, AutoModelForCausalLM # Load custom CUDA kernel from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #define CHUNK_SIZE 64 __global__ void fused_attention_forward(...) { ... } torch::Tensor attention_forward(torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor key, torch::Tensor value) { ... return output; } ''' cpp_source = 'torch::Tensor attention_forward(torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor key, torch::Tensor value);' custom_ops = load_inline( name='custom_attention', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['attention_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=True ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() # Load reference model to extract weights model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) # Extract decoder components decoder = model.model.decoder # Embeddings self.embed_tokens = decoder.embed_tokens self.embed_positions = decoder.embed_positions # Layers self.layers = nn.ModuleList() for layer in decoder.layers: # Extract attention weights # ... concatenate q, k, v ... # Extract FFN weights # ... # Store as a module or dict # Final layer norm self.final_layer_norm = decoder.final_layer_norm # LM head self.lm_head = model.lm_head def forward(self, input_ids): with torch.inference_mode(): # Embedding # For each layer: attention + FFN # Final layer norm + LM head return logits ``` Hmm, I just realized that extracting weights and implementing the forward manually is quite complex and error-prone. What if I just monkey-patch the model's attention to use my custom kernel? Let me think about this: ```python class CustomOPTAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, original_attn): super().__init__() self.q_proj = original_attn.q_proj self.k_proj = original_attn.k_proj self.v_proj = original_attn.v_proj self.out_proj = original_attn.out_proj self.num_heads = original_attn.num_heads self.head_dim = original_attn.head_dim def forward(self, hidden_states, ...): query = self.q_proj(hidden_states) key = self.k_proj(hidden_states) value = self.v_proj(hidden_states) # Reshape query = query.view(...).transpose(1, 2) key = key.view(...).transpose(1, 2) value = value.view(...).transpose(1, 2) # Call custom attention kernel attn_output = custom_ops.attention_forward(query, key, value) # Reshape and project attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2).reshape(...) attn_output = self.out_proj(attn_output) return attn_output, None, None ``` Then in `__init__`: ```python for layer in self.model.model.decoder.layers: layer.self_attn = CustomOPTAttention(layer.self_attn) ``` This way, I keep the rest of the HF model intact and only replace the attention computation. The model forward still goes through all the HF code (layer norms, residual connections, etc.), but the attention uses my custom kernel. Pros: - Less code, less error-prone - Rest of the model exactly matches HF behavior - Easier to implement correctly Cons: - Less opportunity for optimization outside attention - Still pays HF Transformers overhead Actually, the HF overhead might not be that significant for a forward pass. The attention optimization is the main custom kernel requirement. But wait, will this be faster than the reference? If the only change is using a custom attention kernel instead of PyTorch's default matmul+softmax+matmul, the speedup depends on: 1. How much time the reference spends on attention 2. How much faster our custom kernel is Let me estimate. For the reference: - Attention QKV projections: 3 matmuls × 17.2B FLOPs each = ~1.5ms at peak (if compute bound) - Attention scores + softmax + output: 2 matmuls + 1 softmax - Q@K^T: [8*32, 512, 64] @ [8*32, 64, 512] - PyTorch might batch this as [256, 512, 64] @ [256, 64, 512] - This is 256 * 512 * 512 * 2 * 64 FLOPs? No, for batched matmul it's batch * 2 * m * n * k = 256 * 2 * 512 * 512 * 64 = 8.6 GFLOPs - At 67 TFLOPS: ~0.13ms - Softmax: memory bandwidth bound, 256 * 512 * 512 * 4 bytes read+write = 268MB → ~0.08ms - @V: same as Q@K^T = 0.13ms - Total attention computation: ~0.34ms per layer - Output projection: 0.5ms per layer - FFN: ~6ms per layer So attention (Q@K^T, softmax, @V) is only ~0.34ms out of ~7ms per layer. Like: ```cuda // Input: Q, K, V, residual, out_proj_weight, out_proj_bias // Output: hidden_states (after attention + output projection + residual) ``` This would: 1. Compute attention (fused) 2. Multiply by output projection weight 3. Add output projection bias 4. Add residual 5. Return result This saves: - Writing attention output to global memory (33.5MB) - Reading attention output for output projection (33.5MB) - Writing output projection result (33.5MB) - Reading output projection result for residual add (33.5MB) Total savings: ~134MB per layer, or ~3.2GB for 24 layers. At 3.35 TB/s: ~1ms saved. But implementing the output projection (GEMM) in CUDA is very complex. We'd need to call cuBLAS from our kernel or implement a mini-GEMM. Neither is clean. What if we use a different approach: our custom attention kernel returns the attention output in a pre-allocated tensor. Then we use PyTorch for the output projection. We still save the attention intermediate traffic. OK, I'll keep the scope manageable: custom attention kernel + manual Python implementation. Let me think about how to correctly extract and use the model weights. In the HuggingFace OPT model: - `model.model.decoder.embed_tokens.weight`: [vocab_size, hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.embed_positions.weight`: [max_position_embeddings + 2, hidden_size] (OPT uses learned positional embeddings with offset) - For each layer i: - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn_layer_norm.weight`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn_layer_norm.bias`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn.q_proj.weight`: [hidden_size, hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn.q_proj.bias`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn.k_proj.weight`: [hidden_size, hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn.k_proj.bias`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn.v_proj.weight`: [hidden_size, hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn.v_proj.bias`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn.out_proj.weight`: [hidden_size, hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].self_attn.out_proj.bias`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].final_layer_norm.weight`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].final_layer_norm.bias`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].fc1.weight`: [ffn_dim, hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].fc1.bias`: [ffn_dim] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].fc2.weight`: [hidden_size, ffn_dim] - `model.model.decoder.layers[i].fc2.bias`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.final_layer_norm.weight`: [hidden_size] - `model.model.decoder.final_layer_norm.bias`: [hidden_size] - `model.lm_head.weight`: [vocab_size, hidden_size] Wait, does OPT share embeddings with the LM head? Now let me think about the actual code to write. Here's my plan for `ModelNew.__init__`: ```python def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() self.config = config # Load pretrained model ref_model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) # Extract weights decoder = ref_model.model.decoder # We don't need to keep the full reference model, just extract weights self.embed_tokens = decoder.embed_tokens self.embed_positions = decoder.embed_positions # Store layer parameters self.num_layers = config.num_hidden_layers self.hidden_size = config.hidden_size self.num_heads = config.num_attention_heads self.head_dim = self.hidden_size // self.num_heads self.ffn_dim = config.ffn_dim self.layers = nn.ModuleList() for layer in decoder.layers: layer_params = nn.Module() # Layer norms layer_params.self_attn_layer_norm = layer.self_attn_layer_norm layer_params.final_layer_norm = layer.final_layer_norm # QKV projections (concatenated) q_w = layer.self_attn.q_proj.weight k_w = layer.self_attn.k_proj.weight v_w = layer.self_attn.v_proj.weight qkv_w = torch.cat([q_w, k_w, v_w], dim=0) layer_params.qkv_weight = nn.Parameter(qkv_w) q_b = layer.self_attn.q_proj.bias k_b = layer.self_attn.k_proj.bias v_b = layer.self_attn.v_proj.bias qkv_b = torch.cat([q_b, k_b, v_b], dim=0) layer_params.qkv_bias = nn.Parameter(qkv_b) # Output projection layer_params.out_proj_weight = nn.Parameter(layer.self_attn.out_proj.weight) layer_params.out_proj_bias = nn.Parameter(layer.self_attn.out_proj.bias) # FFN layer_params.fc1_weight = nn.Parameter(layer.fc1.weight) layer_params.fc1_bias = nn.Parameter(layer.fc1.bias) layer_params.fc2_weight = nn.Parameter(layer.fc2.weight) layer_params.fc2_bias = nn.Parameter(layer.fc2.bias) self.layers.append(layer_params) self.final_layer_norm = decoder.final_layer_norm self.lm_head = ref_model.lm_head # Move to CUDA self.cuda() ``` Wait, I don't need to explicitly move to CUDA. The weights are already on CUDA when extracted from the model, if the model was loaded on CUDA. But `AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained` typically loads on CPU unless specified. Let me check if we should move to device. Actually, PyTorch usually keeps the model on CPU initially. The evaluation framework likely moves the model to CUDA before running. So we should register the parameters properly (using `nn.Parameter` or buffers) so that `.to(device)` and `.cuda()` work correctly. For `nn.Parameter`, it will be registered as a parameter and participate in `.to(device)` calls. For `nn.Embedding` and `nn.LayerNorm` and `nn.Linear`, these are modules that contain their own parameters and will also move correctly. OK, for the forward pass: ```python def forward(self, input_ids): with torch.inference_mode(): batch_size, seq_len = input_ids.shape device = input_ids.device # Embedding inputs_embeds = self.embed_tokens(input_ids) pos_ids = torch.arange(seq_len, dtype=torch.long, device=device).unsqueeze(0) pos_embeds = self.embed_positions(pos_ids) hidden_states = inputs_embeds + pos_embeds # Process each layer for layer in self.layers: # Self-attention residual = hidden_states hidden_states = layer.self_attn_layer_norm(hidden_states) # QKV projection qkv = F.linear(hidden_states, layer.qkv_weight, layer.qkv_bias) q, k, v = qkv.chunk(3, dim=-1) # Reshape for multi-head attention # hidden_states: [batch, seq, hidden] # We want: [batch, num_heads, seq, head_dim] q = q.view(batch_size, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) k = k.view(batch_size, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) v = v.view(batch_size, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) # Custom attention attn_output = custom_ops.attention_forward(q, k, v) # Reshape back attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().view(batch_size, seq_len, self.hidden_size) # Output projection hidden_states = F.linear(attn_output, layer.out_proj_weight, layer.out_proj_bias) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states # FFN residual = hidden_states hidden_states = layer.final_layer_norm(hidden_states) hidden_states = F.linear(hidden_states, layer.fc1_weight, layer.fc1_bias) hidden_states = F.gelu(hidden_states) hidden_states = F.linear(hidden_states, layer.fc2_weight, layer.fc2_bias) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states # Final layer norm and LM head hidden_states = self.final_layer_norm(hidden_states) logits = self.lm_head(hidden_states) return logits ``` Wait, there are some important things to check: 1. Does OPT use GELU or ReLU? Looking at the code, OPT uses GELU by default. 2. Does the attention mechanism use any scaling? Yes, `1/sqrt(head_dim)`. 3. Does OPT use dropout? During inference, dropout is disabled if `self.training=False`. 4. Are the positional embeddings offset? For OPT, `embed_positions` might have an offset because the position indices start at 2 (due to padding tokens in the original tokenizer). Let me check. In OPT's `embed_positions`: ```python class OPTLearnedPositionalEmbedding(nn.Embedding): def forward(self, attention_mask, past_key_values_length=0): positions = (torch.cumsum(attention_mask, dim=1).type_as(attention_mask) * attention_mask).long() - 1 return super().forward(positions + self.offset) ``` Wait, but `attention_mask` is typically None for a standard forward. In `OPTDecoder.forward`: ```python if inputs_embeds is None: inputs_embeds = self.embed_tokens(input_ids) if attention_mask is None: attention_mask = torch.ones(inputs_embeds.shape[:2], dtype=torch.bool, device=inputs_embeds.device) pos_embeds = self.embed_positions(attention_mask, past_key_values_length) ``` Hmm, `embed_positions` takes `attention_mask` as input, not just position IDs. The standard call is: ```python pos_embeds = self.embed_positions(attention_mask, past_key_values_length=0) ``` For our manual implementation, we need to match this correctly. If we're passing `input_ids` (with no padding), the attention mask would be all ones, and the positions would be `torch.arange(seq_len) + past_key_values_length`. Looking at `OPTLearnedPositionalEmbedding.forward`: ```python def forward(self, attention_mask: torch.LongTensor, past_key_values_length: int = 0): attention_mask = attention_mask.long() positions = (torch.cumsum(attention_mask, dim=1).type_as(attention_mask) * attention_mask).long() - 1 positions = positions[:, past_key_values_length:] return super().forward(positions + self.offset) ``` For all-ones attention mask: `cumsum(ones)` gives `[1, 2, 3, ..., seq_len]`. Multiplying by ones keeps it. Then subtract 1: `[0, 1, 2, ..., seq_len-1]`. Then add `self.offset`. For OPT, `self.offset` is 2 (from the config `padding_idx=1`, and `offset=padding_idx+1=2`). So positions would be `[2, 3, 4, ..., seq_len+1]`. And `self.embed_positions` is an `nn.Embedding` of size `[max_position_embeddings + self.offset + 1, embed_dim]`. For our manual implementation: ```python attention_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, seq_len, dtype=torch.bool, device=device) positions = torch.cumsum(attention_mask.long(), dim=1) * attention_mask.long() - 1 pos_embeds = self.embed_positions(positions) ``` Or more simply, since all are ones: ```python positions = torch.arange(seq_len, device=device).unsqueeze(0).expand(batch_size, -1) pos_embeds = self.embed_positions(positions + 2) ``` Actually wait, I should check what `self.offset` is for the specific model. It might be stored as a buffer or attribute. Looking at the initialization: ```python class OPTLearnedPositionalEmbedding(nn.Embedding): def __init__(self, num_embeddings: int, embedding_dim: int, padding_idx: int, offset: int): super().__init__(num_embeddings + offset, embedding_dim, padding_idx=padding_idx) self.offset = offset self.padding_idx = padding_idx ``` For OPT, `offset` is typically `padding_idx + 1`. With `padding_idx=1`, `offset=2`. So: ```python positions = torch.arange(seq_len, dtype=torch.long, device=device).unsqueeze(0).expand(batch_size, -1) pos_embeds = self.embed_positions(positions + self.embed_positions_offset) ``` OK that should work. Another thing: the attention mask used inside the attention mechanism. In our custom kernel, we implement causal masking. But does the HF model use a causal mask by default? Looking at `OPTForCausalLM.forward`: ```python output_attentions = output_attentions if output_attentions is not None else self.config.output_attentions output_hidden_states = output_hidden_states if output_hidden_states is not None else self.config.output_hidden_states return_dict = return_dict if return_dict is not None else self.config.use_return_dict # decoder outputs consists of (dec_features, layer_state, dec_hidden, dec_attn) outputs = self.model.decoder( input_ids=input_ids, attention_mask=attention_mask, ... ) ``` And in `OPTDecoder.prepare_decoder_attention_mask`: ```python combined_attention_mask = _prepare_4d_causal_attention_mask( attention_mask, input_shape, inputs_embeds, past_key_values_length ) ``` So yes, a causal mask is created and passed to the decoder layers. Our custom kernel with causal masking should match this. One subtlety: the causal mask in HF adds `-inf` to the upper triangle, which makes `softmax` output 0. In our kernel, we skip computing those positions (or set them to a very negative number). We need to make sure the numerical result is the same. In our kernel, for position i and key j > i, we either: 1. Set the score to a very large negative number, so exp(score) ≈ 0 2. Or skip them entirely Skipping them is numerically cleaner because we don't include them in the max or sum. This gives the exact same result as masking with -inf. Actually, let me verify. If the causal mask sets attention to -inf for j > i, then: - The softmax max is computed only over j <= i - The softmax sum is computed only over j <= i - This is exactly what our kernel does! So our kernel should produce identical results to the reference (within fp32 precision). Wait, there's another subtlety. In the reference, the attention computation is: ```python attn_weights = torch.matmul(query_states, key_states.transpose(-1, -2)) * self.scaling ``` Our kernel computes: ```python score = dot(Q[i], K[j]) * scale ``` These should be mathematically equivalent, but there might be slight differences in the order of operations (e.g., how the dot product is accumulated). In FP32, the difference should be very small (within 1e-4 tolerance). Actually, I should be more careful. The reference uses `torch.matmul` which might use different accumulation patterns than our manual loop. For example, `torch.matmul` might use block-wise accumulation with different ordering. To maximize accuracy, I should try to match the reference's computation order. But that's difficult to guarantee. The tolerance is 1e-4, which is quite strict. I'll need to test carefully. One potential issue: our kernel uses `float` (FP32) accumulation in a loop: ```cuda float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_vec[d] * k_smem[j * head_dim + d]; } ``` cuBLAS might do the dot product in a different order (e.g., pairwise summation, or using FMA operations in a specific order). This could lead to slightly different results. For a dot product of 64 elements, the maximum difference due to reordering is roughly `64 * eps * max_value` where `eps = 1.19e-7` for FP32. If the values are around 1.0, the error might be up to ~7.6e-6 per dot product. After multiple layers, this could accumulate. But with tolerance 1e-4, it should still be fine for the dot product differences. The main issue is whether the overall layer outputs match. Let me also think about another source of difference: our online softmax uses a mathematically stable algorithm, but the intermediate values (max, sum_exp) might differ slightly from the reference. Actually, the reference computes: 1. `attn_weights = Q @ K^T / sqrt(d)` 2. `attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask` (adds 0 or -inf) 3. `attn_weights = softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1)` Standard softmax in PyTorch: ```python max_val = torch.max(attn_weights, dim=-1, keepdim=True)[0] exp_weights = torch.exp(attn_weights - max_val) sum_exp = torch.sum(exp_weights, dim=-1, keepdim=True) softmax = exp_weights / sum_exp ``` Our online algorithm computes the same mathematically but incrementally. The result should be identical within rounding error. For FP32, the difference should be negligible (well under 1e-4). OK, I'm fairly confident the numerical match should be fine. Let me now write the actual code. Wait, before writing, let me also think about whether I should write any other custom kernels. For example: **Kernel 2: Fused GELU + bias + residual add?** No, because GELU is applied after FC1, and then FC2 comes after, so we can't fuse FC1's GELU with FC2. **Kernel 3: Elementwise operations** A simple kernel that adds bias and residual in one pass: ```cuda __global__ void add_bias_residual(float* out, const float* input, const float* bias, const float* residual, int hidden_size) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx < hidden_size) { out[idx] = input[idx] + bias[idx] + residual[idx]; } } ``` But PyTorch's elementwise ops are already very efficient. The main benefit of fusion is saving memory traffic. For bias + residual: - Without: linear writes output (33.5MB), bias_add reads/writes (67MB), residual_add reads/writes (100MB) - total 200.5MB traffic for operations on 33.5MB of data - Wait, that doesn't seem right. Actually, for PyTorch: 1. `output = F.linear(input, weight, bias)` - this computes the matmul and adds bias in one cuBLAS call if possible, or in two steps 2. `output = output + residual` - elementwise addition For step 1, if cuBLAS can fuse bias, it's one kernel. If not, it's matmul then bias add. For step 2, it's another kernel that reads output and residual, then writes result. If we could fuse all three: matmul + bias + residual, we'd save memory traffic. But again, implementing the matmul part in CUDA is hard. Actually, I just learned that `torch.addmm` can compute `alpha * (input @ mat2) + beta * input_bias`. If we set `input_bias = residual` and `beta = 1`, we can fuse the output projection with the residual! Wait, let me check: `torch.addmm(input, mat1, mat2, beta=1, alpha=1)` computes `beta * input + alpha * (mat1 @ mat2)`. So for `output = residual + attention_output @ W_out`: ```python output = torch.addmm(residual, attn_output, out_proj_weight.t(), beta=1.0, alpha=1.0) ``` Hmm, but `F.linear` computes `input @ weight.t() + bias`. If we want to add residual too, we can do: ```python # Compute attention output projection without bias proj = torch.mm(attn_output, out_proj_weight.t()) # Then add bias and residual output = proj + out_proj_bias + residual ``` This still requires two kernels (matmul, then add). With `addmm`: ```python output = torch.addmm(out_proj_bias + residual, attn_output, out_proj_weight.t(), beta=1.0, alpha=1.0) ``` Wait, no. `addmm(bias_residual, attn_output, weight.t())` computes `bias_residual + attn_output @ weight.t()`. So if `bias_residual = out_proj_bias + residual`, we get: ```python output = (out_proj_bias + residual) + attn_output @ out_proj_weight.t() ``` Which is what we want! And it's a single GEMM call with fused bias! But the issue is that `out_proj_bias` is [hidden_size] and `residual` is [batch*seq, hidden_size]. Broadcasting works, but creating `bias_residual = out_proj_bias + residual` first requires a memory allocation and a kernel launch. Actually, if we do `torch.addmm(residual, attn_output, weight.t(), beta=1, alpha=1)` and the bias is separate, there's no easy way to fuse all three without precomputing. Wait, `torch.addmm` supports the `input` argument being 1D, in which case it's broadcast: ```python output = torch.addmm(bias, mat1, mat2) # Computes: output = bias + mat1 @ mat2 # Where bias is broadcast over the batch dimension ``` This is actually what `F.linear` uses internally for the bias addition! So for `output = residual + attn_output @ weight.t() + bias`, we can't directly use `addmm` because `addmm` only has one "bias" term. We'd need: ```python output = torch.addmm(bias, attn_output, weight.t()) # Computes bias + attn_output @ weight.t() output = output + residual # Still need this second op ``` Unless we do: ```python # Pre-add bias to residual (requires memory) output = torch.addmm(residual + bias, attn_output, weight.t()) ``` But `residual + bias` requires a kernel launch and temporary memory. Hmm, actually we can do this without extra memory: ```python output = residual.clone() # Allocates new memory output.addmm_(attn_output, weight.t(), beta=1.0, alpha=1.0) if bias is not None: output.add_(bias) ``` Wait, `addmm_(input, mat1, mat2, beta, alpha)` modifies `input` in place. So: ```python output = residual.clone() output.addmm_(attn_output, weight.t(), beta=1.0, alpha=1.0) if bias is not None: output.add_(bias) ``` This does: 1. Clone residual: allocates output, copies residual (read+write = 2 * 33.5MB) 2. `addmm_`: reads output, attn_output, weight; writes output. But this might be fused? The in-place addmm might compute `output = beta * output + alpha * (attn_output @ weight.t())`. If beta=1, it's `output = output + attn_output @ weight.t()`. So the bias isn't included. 3. `add_` (bias): reads output and bias, writes output So total extra memory traffic for the clone is 33.5MB. Not great. Actually, we could just allocate output and copy residual: ```python output = residual.clone() # One kernel launch output = torch.addmm(output, attn_output, weight.t(), beta=1.0, alpha=1.0) # Fused? ``` Wait, `torch.addmm` returns a new tensor. If we want to modify in-place, we use `addmm_`. But even then, the read of the old output tensor and write of new values are the same memory, so the cache might optimize it. Actually, let me reconsider: can `torch.addmm` with `beta=1, alpha=1` and `input=residual` compute `residual + attn_output @ weight.t()` without a clone? If we do: ```python output = torch.addmm(residual, attn_output, weight.t(), beta=1.0, alpha=1.0) ``` Does PyTorch allocate a new tensor for output? Yes. But `residual` is still read, and output is written. The memory traffic is the same as if we did `output = residual + torch.mm(...)`. But it's one kernel call instead of two, which saves launch overhead. Actually wait, does `torch.addmm` compute everything in one cuBLAS call? Looking at the PyTorch source, `addmm` calls `cublasSgemm` or similar with appropriate parameters to compute `beta*C + alpha*(A@B)`. So yes, it's a single cuBLAS call! But this still only handles one bias/offset term. For two terms (residual + bias), we'd need either: 1. `torch.addmm(residual + bias, attn_output, weight.t())` - but this requires computing residual + bias first 2. Or just use `F.linear` then add residual: two kernel calls The overhead of an extra elementwise kernel is small compared to the GEMM. So I'll just use the simpler approach: ```python hidden_states = F.linear(attn_output, out_proj_weight, out_proj_bias) hidden_states = hidden_states + residual ``` This is clear and the performance difference is minimal. OK HERE IS THE CODE: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from transformers import AutoConfig, AutoModelForCausalLM cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #define CHUNK_SIZE 64 __global__ void fused_attention_forward( const float* __restrict__ query, const float* __restrict__ key, const float* __restrict__ value, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { int batch = blockIdx.x / num_heads; int head = blockIdx.x % num_heads; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; int bh_offset = ((batch * num_heads + head) * seq_len * head_dim); query += bh_offset; key += bh_offset; value += bh_offset; output += bh_offset; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + CHUNK_SIZE * head_dim; int positions_per_thread = (seq_len + num_threads - 1) / num_threads; for (int p = 0; p < positions_per_thread; p++) { int i = tid + p * num_threads; bool valid = (i < seq_len); float q_vec[64]; float o[64]; float m = -1e20f; float l = 0.0f; if (valid) { for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_vec[d] = query[i * head_dim + d]; o[d] = 0.0f; } } for (int chunk_start = 0; chunk_start < seq_len; chunk_start += CHUNK_SIZE) { int chunk_size = min(CHUNK_SIZE, seq_len - chunk_start); // Collaborative load of K and V chunks int total_load = chunk_size * head_dim; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_load; idx += num_threads) { int j = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; int seq_j = chunk_start + j; k_smem[j * head_dim + d] = key[seq_j * head_dim + d]; v_smem[j * head_dim + d] = value[seq_j * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); if (valid) { float m_chunk = -1e20f; float s[64]; // Compute scores for this chunk for (int j = 0; j < chunk_size; j++) { int seq_j = chunk_start + j; if (seq_j > i) { s[j] = -1e20f; continue; } float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_vec[d] * k_smem[j * head_dim + d]; } s[j] = dot * scale; if (s[j] > m_chunk) m_chunk = s[j]; } float m_new = fmaxf(m, m_chunk); float exp_m_old = expf(m - m_new); float l_new = l * exp_m_old; for (int j = 0; j < chunk_size; j++) { l_new += expf(s[j] - m_new); } for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { o[d] *= exp_m_old; } for (int j = 0; j < chunk_size; j++) { float p = expf(s[j] - m_new); for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { o[d] += p * v_smem[j * head_dim + d]; } } m = m_new; l = l_new; } __syncthreads(); } if (valid) { float inv_l = 1.0f / l; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { output[i * head_dim + d] = o[d] * inv_l; } } } } torch::Tensor attention_forward( torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor key, torch::Tensor value ) { int batch_size = query.size(0); int num_heads = query.size(1); int seq_len = query.size(2); int head_dim = query.size(3); auto output = torch::empty_like(query); int num_blocks = batch_size * num_heads; int num_threads = 128; size_t smem_size = 2 * CHUNK_SIZE * head_dim * sizeof(float); fused_attention_forward<<<num_blocks, num_threads, smem_size>>>( query.data_ptr<float>(), key.data_ptr<float>(), value.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim ); return output; } """ cpp_source = "torch::Tensor attention_forward(torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor key, torch::Tensor value);" custom_ops = load_inline( name="custom_attention", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["attention_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() self.config = config # Load the reference model to extract weights ref_model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) ref_model.eval() decoder = ref_model.model.decoder # Embeddings self.embed_tokens = decoder.embed_tokens self.embed_positions = decoder.embed_positions self.embed_positions_offset = getattr(decoder.embed_positions, 'offset', 2) # Layer parameters self.num_layers = config.num_hidden_layers self.hidden_size = config.hidden_size self.num_heads = config.num_attention_heads self.head_dim = self.hidden_size // self.num_heads self.ffn_dim = config.ffn_dim # Extract layer params self.layers = nn.ModuleList() for layer in decoder.layers: layer_params = nn.Module() # Layer norms layer_params.self_attn_layer_norm = layer.self_attn_layer_norm layer_params.final_layer_norm = layer.final_layer_norm # Concatenate Q, K, V weights and biases qkv_w = torch.cat([ layer.self_attn.q_proj.weight, layer.self_attn.k_proj.weight, layer.self_attn.v_proj.weight ], dim=0) layer_params.qkv_weight = nn.Parameter(qkv_w.contiguous()) qkv_b = torch.cat([ layer.self_attn.q_proj.bias, layer.self_attn.k_proj.bias, layer.self_attn.v_proj.bias ], dim=0) layer_params.qkv_bias = nn.Parameter(qkv_b.contiguous()) # Output projection layer_params.out_proj_weight = nn.Parameter(layer.self_attn.out_proj.weight.contiguous()) layer_params.out_proj_bias = nn.Parameter(layer.self_attn.out_proj.bias.contiguous()) # FFN layer_params.fc1_weight = nn.Parameter(layer.fc1.weight.contiguous()) layer_params.fc1_bias = nn.Parameter(layer.fc1.bias.contiguous()) layer_params.fc2_weight = nn.Parameter(layer.fc2.weight.contiguous()) layer_params.fc2_bias = nn.Parameter(layer.fc2.bias.contiguous()) self.layers.append(layer_params) # Final layer norm self.final_layer_norm = decoder.final_layer_norm # LM head self.lm_head = ref_model.lm_head # Clean up reference model (optional, to save memory) del ref_model def forward(self, input_ids): with torch.inference_mode(): batch_size, seq_len = input_ids.shape device = input_ids.device # Embeddings input_embeds = self.embed_tokens(input_ids) positions = torch.arange(seq_len, dtype=torch.long, device=device).unsqueeze(0) pos_embeds = self.embed_positions(positions + self.embed_positions_offset) hidden_states = input_embeds + pos_embeds # Process layers for layer in self.layers: # Self-attention with residual residual = hidden_states hidden_states = layer.self_attn_layer_norm(hidden_states) qkv = F.linear(hidden_states, layer.qkv_weight, layer.qkv_bias) q, k, v = qkv.chunk(3, dim=-1) # Reshape to [batch, num_heads, seq, head_dim] q = q.view(batch_size, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) k = k.view(batch_size, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) v = v.view(batch_size, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) # Custom attention kernel attn_out = custom_ops.attention_forward(q, k, v) # Reshape back attn_out = attn_out.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().view(batch_size, seq_len, self.hidden_size) hidden_states = F.linear(attn_out, layer.out_proj_weight, layer.out_proj_bias) hidden_states = hidden_states + residual # FFN with residual residual = hidden_states hidden_states = layer.final_layer_norm(hidden_states) hidden_states = F.linear(hidden_states, layer.fc1_weight, layer.fc1_bias) hidden_states = F.gelu(hidden_states) hidden_states = F.linear(hidden_states, layer.fc2_weight, layer.fc2_bias) hidden_states = hidden_states + residual # Final norm + LM head hidden_states = self.final_layer_norm(hidden_states) logits = self.lm_head(hidden_states) return logits ``` Wait, I just realized there are some issues with this code: 1. **Position embeddings**: `self.embed_positions(positions + self.embed_positions_offset)` - If the tensor isn't contiguous after transpose, our kernel would read wrong data! To fix this, we should make sure Q, K, V are contiguous before passing to the kernel. We can use `.contiguous()` after transpose: ```python q = q.view(batch_size, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2).contiguous() ``` This adds a memory copy, but ensures correctness. Then `transpose(1, 2)` gives [batch, num_heads, seq, head_dim]. For a tensor of shape [B, S, H, D] with contiguous memory (strides [S*H*D, H*D, D, 1]), after transpose to [B, H, S, D], the strides become [S*H*D, D, H*D, 1]. This is NOT contiguous. Also, let me think about whether the kernel might have issues with memory alignment or coalescing. When loading `k_smem` and `v_smem`, the collaborative load pattern is: ```cuda for (int idx = tid; idx < total_load; idx += num_threads) { int j = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; k_smem[j * head_dim + d] = key[seq_j * head_dim + d]; } ``` The global memory access is strided: - Thread 0 accesses `key[0], key[1], key[2], ...` (for the first key vector) - Thread 1 accesses `key[head_dim], key[head_dim+1], ...` - etc. Actually, `idx` increments by 1, so `j = idx / head_dim` and `d = idx % head_dim`. Thread `tid` accesses `key[j * head_dim + d]` where j and d depend on `idx`. For `tid=0`: idx=0,1,2,... accesses key[0], key[1], key[2],... sequentially. This is fully coalesced (consecutive threads access consecutive memory). Wait, let me check: - Thread 0: idx=0 → j=0, d=0 → key[0 * head_dim + 0] = key[0] - Thread 1: idx=1 → j=0, d=1 → key[0 * head_dim + 1] = key[1] - ... - Thread head_dim-1: idx=head_dim-1 → j=0, d=head_dim-1 → key[head_dim-1] - Thread head_dim: idx=head_dim → j=1, d=0 → key[head_dim] - ... So consecutive threads access consecutive elements in the key tensor. The key tensor for one (batch, head) has shape [seq_len, head_dim] and is stored in row-major order. So consecutive elements along the last dimension are contiguous in memory. This means consecutive threads access contiguous memory! Perfect coalescing. Great, the memory access pattern is optimal. For the output write: ```cuda output[i * head_dim + d] = o[d] * inv_l; ``` Thread tid handles position i = tid + p * num_threads. Position i writes to `output[i * head_dim + d]` for d=0..head_dim-1. For p=0, thread tid writes to position tid. Consecutive threads write to consecutive positions, and within each position, they write to consecutive d values. But different positions are separated by `head_dim * sizeof(float)` bytes. Actually, each thread writes an entire vector of `head_dim` floats. Consecutive threads write to `output[0 * head_dim + d]`, `output[1 * head_dim + d]`, etc. These vectors are separated by `head_dim * 4` bytes. This is a strided write pattern where each thread writes a contiguous block, and blocks are separated by `head_dim * 4` bytes. The writes within each block are coalesced, but consecutive threads don't write to consecutive addresses across thread boundaries. However, modern GPUs handle this well because each thread writes a contiguous sequence, and within a warp, threads write to addresses that are strided but regular. The memory controller can merge these into efficient transactions. Actually, for better coalescing, it would be ideal if consecutive threads wrote to adjacent memory. But with our design (each thread handling one position), it's not possible without restructuring. This strided write is common in attention kernels and is generally not a bottleneck because the compute dominates. OK, I think the code is good. Let me write it all out.
